Quranic Proofs That Faith and Islam Are One
Maturidi presents two Quranic proofs for the identity of īmān and Islām.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the verses from Surah al-Hujurat and Surah Al-Imran to prove that the reality of faith (īmān) and Islam is one.

Core Idea
The Quran treats 'faith' and 'Islam' as the same reality.

In this lesson, we examine the first part of Maturidi's proof. He cites two Quranic passages: one about the Bedouins claiming to have submitted, and another about disbelief after faith. Both show that faith and Islam refer to the same thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic proofs
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two verses Maturidi quotes and how he draws conclusions from them.
Pay attention to the logical flow: verse, then argument, then conclusion.
2
Analyze the first proof from al-Hujurat
The Bedouins say they have submitted (aslamnā). God says 'If you are truthful' – implying their submission is not true faith. Yet God still calls them Muslims. Maturidi argues this shows that the reality of Islam is faith, not just outward show.
3
Analyze the second proof from Al-Imran
God says no religion other than Islam is accepted, and then He describes those who disbelieved after faith (īmān) as 'changers of the religion of Islam'. This shows that faith and Islam are the same religion.
The verse equates turning away from faith with turning away from Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل ذلك الأمر الذي ذكر أن الله قال في آخر السورة : يمنون عليك أن أسلموا قل لا تمنوا على إسلامكم ﴾ [الحجرات، ١٧/٤٩]، ولو كان الإسلام ما أظهروا فقد كان ذلك ، كيف قال : إن كنتم صادقين ﴾ [الحجرات، ١٧/٤٩]. ثم فيه أنه جعلهم مسلمين كما قال " ، أن هدوا للإيمان لا بما أظهروا . ثبت أن الإيمان هو الإسلام. وكذلك قال: ومن يبتغ غير الإسلام دينا فلن يقبل منه ﴾ [آل عمران، ٨٥/٣]، ثم بين ذلك الدين الذي هو الإسلام وقال : كيف يهدى الله قوما كفروا بعد إيمانهم ﴾ [آل عمران، ٨٦/٣] الآية، ، صيّر ذلك لم يُقبل دينا غير الإيمان الذي وصف، ثم جعلهم مبدلين دين الإسلام بالكفر بعد الإيمان ليعلموا أنهما واحد. مع ما كان فيما تقدم كفاية من قوله : قولوا آمنا بالله وما أنزل إلينا إلى قوله : مسلمون . والأصل عندنا أن الأسماء إنما جعلت لمعرفة أهلها فيما أُريدوا بأمور جُعِلت لهم وعليهم، وفيما وعدوا وأُوعدوا. ثم لم يكن أحد يخير فيما جرى آية الذكر باسم الإيمان أو الإسلام ممن ينتحل دين الإسلام - في اقتضاء الذكر إياه من حيث الاسم. ثبت أن حقيقتهما واحد وأن من يروم التفريق بينهما من بعيد يخترع. ولا قوة إلا بالله تعالى. وصلى الله على سيدنا محمد وآله الطيبين الطاهرين
Proof of that matter which He mentioned: that God said at the end of the surah: 'They regard it as a favor to you that they have submitted. Say: Do not count your submission as a favor to me...' [al-Hujurāt 49:17]. And if Islam were what they outwardly showed, then that would have been the case. How did He say: 'If you are truthful' [al-Hujurāt 49:17]. Then in that verse He made them Muslims as He said, and that they were guided to faith (īmān), not because of what they outwardly showed. So it is established that faith (īmān) is Islam. Likewise He said: 'Whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will not be accepted from him' [Āl ʿImrān 3:85]. Then He explained that religion which is Islam and said: 'How will Allah guide a people who disbelieved after their belief (īmān)' [Āl ʿImrān 3:86] – the verse. He made it such that no religion is accepted other than the faith (īmān) which He described, and then He made them changers of the religion of Islam by disbelief after faith (īmān) to make them know that they are one. Along with what was sufficient previously from His statement: 'Say we believe (āmannā) in God and what has been sent down to us...' up to His statement: 'and we are Muslims (muslimūn)' [al-Baqarah 2:136]. And the principle with us is that names are only made for knowing their people regarding matters that are made for them and against them, and regarding what they are promised and warned. Then no one who professes the religion of Islam is given a choice in the mention of the name of faith (īmān) or Islam – in the requirement of the mention thereof in terms of the name. It is established that their reality is one, and whoever attempts to differentiate between them is far off and invents. And there is no power except with God Almighty. And may God bless our master Muhammad and his pure and virtuous family.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage provides a proof from the Quran that faith (īmān) and Islam are identical. The proof begins by citing a verse from Sūrat al-Ḥujurāt where God rebukes the Bedouins for claiming to have submitted (aslamnā) as a favor; He says 'If you are truthful' – indicating that their submission is not true faith. Yet God still calls them 'Muslims' in the sense of outward submission, but He says they have not truly believed. The argument then points to Āl ʿImrān where God states that no religion other than Islam will be accepted, and then He equates 'disbelieving after faith (īmān)' with changing the religion of Islam, showing that īmān and Islam refer to the same thing. Another verse, al-Baqarah 2:136, combines both īmān and Islam in the same statement. The passage concludes with a principle about names: names like 'īmān' and 'Islam' are given to distinguish people for reward and punishment. Since no one can choose to be called by one or the other in the Quran – both are used for the same people – their reality is one. Those who try to separate them are inventing a distinction. The proof is cumulative, using multiple Quranic examples to establish the unity of īmān and Islam.

Key Terms
إيمان (īmān)
إسلام (Islām)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Bedouin verse proves that Islam and faith are different, because the Bedouins were Muslims but not believers.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the verse actually proves they are one: God calls them Muslims but says they have not believed, showing that true submission (Islam) is faith, and their outward submission was not the true Islam.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion Maturidi draws from the verse 'Whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will not be accepted from him'?
A) That Islam is the only true religion
B) That faith and Islam are two different things
C) That disbelief after faith changes the religion of Islam
D) That the Bedouins were true believers

Key Takeaway
The Quran uses 'faith' and 'Islam' interchangeably; Maturidi demonstrates this through two clear examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated your Islamic practices as a favor to Allah, like the Bedouins? Examine your heart: do you feel entitled because you pray or fast, or do you see these acts as expressions of genuine faith? What would Maturidi's rebuke expose in your daily worship?
?
Maturidi proves that true Islam is identical to faith. Is there a gap between what you outwardly perform and what you inwardly believe? Where is the weakest link in your life—an action without conviction or a conviction without action?
?
The second proof warns that rejecting Islam after faith is equivalent to changing the religion of Islam. Does your commitment to Islam rest on a deep, personal conviction that no trial could shake? If your faith were tested tomorrow, would your submission hold firm?

The Principle of Names and the Unity of Faith and Islam
Maturidi provides a principle about names and concludes that īmān and Islām are identical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle about the purpose of names and how it confirms that faith and Islam are one reality.

Core Idea
Names like 'faith' and 'Islam' are given to identify people for reward or punishment.

After presenting Quranic evidence, Maturidi adds a logical principle: names are given to distinguish people regarding matters that affect their standing with God. Since both 'faith' and 'Islam' are used for the same people in the Quran, their reality must be one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on names
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Focus on the principle Maturidi states about the purpose of names.
Notice that he says 'al-asmā' innamā juʿilat li-maʿrifati ahlihā' – names are made for knowing their people.
2
Understand the logical implication
If faith and Islam were different, they would apply to different people. But in the Quran, the same people are called both believers and Muslims. Therefore, they are the same.
3
Reach the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that whoever tries to separate them is inventing a distinction. This solidifies the unity of faith and Islam.

The Middle Path on Divine Attributes
Presents the Sunni middle position on divine attributes between ta'til and tashbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how both al-Ash'ari and al-Maturidi affirmed divine attributes while rejecting both outright denial (ta'til) and anthropomorphism (tashbih).

Core Idea
God's attributes are real but utterly unlike creation.

Theologians before al-Ash'ari and al-Maturidi fell into two extremes: some denied God's attributes entirely, while others compared them directly to created qualities. This lesson presents the balanced approach of the two great Sunni imams, who affirmed that God possesses attributes in a manner befitting His majesty, without resemblance to any creation. Their method, known as 'the middle path,' became the standard of Sunni orthodoxy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two extremes
Read the first part of the passage to see how al-Ash'ari surveyed the groups that negated attributes (ta'til) and those that likened them to creation (tashbih).
Note the specific groups mentioned: Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, Rafida on one side; Hashwiyya, Mujassima, Mukayyifa on the other.
2
Examine Ash'ari's middle statement
Look at the sentence where al-Ash'ari says 'knowledge not like knowledges, power not like powers...' and notice the formula 'la ka-' (not like).
This is the core of the middle path: affirmation without resemblance.
3
Follow al-Maturidi's agreement
Read the quotations from Kitab al-Tawhid and see how Maturidi uses the same logic, emphasizing that naming and describing God is true from revelation and reason.
He clarifies that the shared name does not imply shared reality.
4
Understand the rejection of tashbih
Focus on Maturidi's statement 'God is one; there is no similitude to Him' and the conclusion that affirming attributes removes, not creates, similarity.
The phrase 'Knower not like knowers' is the key to orthodox tanzih.
5
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider why this balance is crucial: it preserves God's transcendence while maintaining the reality of His attributes taught in revelation.
This middle path became the standard for Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتوسط الأشعري في الصفات فيذكر ابن عساكر أنه نظر في كتب المعتزلة والجهمية والرافضة وأنهم عطلوا وأبطلوا فقالوا: لا علم لله ولا قدرة ولا سمع ولا بصر ولا حياة ولا بقاء ولا إرادة. وقالت الحشوية والمجسمة والمكيفة المحددة: إن الله كالعلوم وقدرة كالقدر وسمعاً كالأسماع وبصراً كالأبصار. فسلك رضي الله عنه طريقة بينهما فقال: إن الله سبحانه وتعالى علماً لا كالعلوم وقدرة لا كالقدر وسمعاً لا كالأسماع وبصراً لا كالأبصار. وكذلك يفعل الماتريدي؛ إذ نقرأ في كتاب التوحيد: ثم الوصف الله بأنه قادر عالم حي كريم جواد والتسمية بها حق من السمع والعقل جميعاً. قوماً وجهوا تلك الأسماء إلى غيره ظنا منهم ... إلا أن في إثبات الاسم تشابهاً بينه وبين كل مسمى... ولكن قد بينا بعد التشابه به لموافقة الاسم، فهو مسمى بما سمى نفسه، موصوف بما وصف به نفسه. ثم يقول الماتريدي في موضع آخر: والله واحد لا شبيه له وإذا ثبت ذلك بطل تقدير جميع ما يضاف إليه من الخلق ويوصف به من الصفات، بما يفهم منه لو أضيف إلى الخلق وفي ذلك ظهور تعنت المشبهة، وذلك سبب الحاد من وصف به الحد إنه ظن به ما احتمله الشاهد ... وليس في إثبات الأسماء وتحقيق الصفات تشابه لكنا [أردنا] به ما يُسقط الشبه من قولنا: عالم لا كالعلماء، وهذا النوع في كل ما نسميه به ونصفه.
Al-Ash'ari takes a middle position regarding the attributes. Ibn 'Asakir mentions that he examined the books of the Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, and Rafida, and that they negated and nullified, saying: There is no knowledge for God, nor power, nor hearing, nor sight, nor life, nor eternity, nor will. And the Hashwiyya, Mujassima, and Mukayyifa al-Muhaddada said: God's knowledge is like [created] knowledges, and His power is like power, and His hearing is like hearings, and His sight like sights. So he, may God be pleased with him, took a way between them and said: Indeed God, glorified and exalted, has knowledge not like knowledges, and power not like powers, and hearing not like hearings, and sight not like sights. And likewise al-Maturidi does, for we read in Kitab al-Tawhid: Then the description of God as Qadir, 'Alim, Hayy, Karim, Jawwad, and the naming with them is true from both revelation and reason. Some people directed those names to other than Him, imagining ... but there is in affirming the name a similarity between it and every named ... but we have explained after [؟] the similarity due to agreement of the name; so He is named by what He named Himself, described by what He described Himself. Then al-Maturidi says in another place: God is one; there is no similitude to Him. And when that is established, the estimation of all that is added to Him from creation and described by attributes—according to what is understood if it were added to creation—is invalid. In that is the manifestation of the obstinacy of the Mushabbiha, and that is the reason for the heresy of those who describe Him with limit, for they assumed about Him what the witnessed [creation] implies ... And there is not in affirming the names and verifying the attributes any similarity; rather we intend by that what removes similitude from our saying: 'Knower not like knowers,' and this type is in everything we name and describe Him with.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage presents the middle position of both al-Ash'ari and al-Maturidi on divine attributes. It first quotes Ibn 'Asakir on Ash'ari's approach: Ash'ari examined three extreme groups: the Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, and Rafida who negated God's attributes (ta'til), and the Hashwiyya, Mujassima, and Mukayyifa who claimed God's attributes are like created attributes (tashbih). Ash'ari took the middle path: God has attributes that are real but not like any created attributes. The passage then shows that Maturidi follows the same method. Two quotations from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid are given. The first affirms that describing God with names like Qadir, 'Alim, etc., is valid by both revelation and reason, and clarifies that there is a similarity in the name itself (the same word is used) but the reality is unlike creation. The second quotation emphasizes that God is one with no similitude; the attributes understood from creation cannot apply to God. Affirming names and attributes entails no tashbih; rather, it removes tashbih by saying 'Knower not like knowers.' The passage thus shows the systematic consistency of the two scholars in affirming divine attributes without anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
التعطيل
التشبيه
علم لا كالعلوم
قدرة لا كالقدر
السمع والعقل
كتاب التوحيد
لا شبيه له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming divine names and attributes implies that God resembles creation (tashbih).
Correction: The passage clarifies that affirming attributes with the phrase 'not like' removes any resemblance. The similarity is only in the name, not the reality.

Checkpoint
What middle statement does al-Ash'ari make regarding God's attributes according to Ibn 'Asakir?
A) God has no attributes
B) God's attributes are exactly like those of creation
C) God has attributes but not like any created attributes
D) Attributes are only metaphorical

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari and al-Maturidi affirmed real divine attributes while insisting they are utterly unlike any created attributes, rejecting both denial and anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Knowing', do your thoughts subtly picture a human-like mind that knows everything? How will you train your heart to affirm 'knowledge not like knowledges' starting today?
?
Is there an attribute of God—His mercy, His justice—that you have unconsciously measured by your own experience, limiting His infinitude? What specific thought patterns do you need to abandon to truly believe He is unlike anything you know?
?
The passage teaches that affirming names and attributes is from both revelation and reason. Are you willing to let revelation discipline your reason when your mind tries to imagine God? What concrete step will you take to purify your understanding of His attributes?

The Middle Position on Divine Speech
Explains Ash'ari's and Maturidi's distinction between eternal speech and created words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between God's eternal speech and created physical expressions according to Ash'ari and Maturidi.

Core Idea
God's eternal attribute of speech is distinct from created letters and sounds.

This lesson examines the middle position of Ash'ari and Maturidi on the nature of God's speech. They avoid the extremes of Mu'tazila and Hashwiyya by distinguishing between the eternal internal speech and the created physical manifestations. The passage from Kitab al-Tawhid outlines this nuanced view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage contrasts three views.
2
Identify the two extreme positions
The passage first mentions the Mu'tazila who say all of God's speech is created, and the Hashwiyya who say even the letters and paper are eternal. Understand the error of each.
These extremes set up the middle position.
3
Understand Ash'ari's middle position
Ash'ari distinguishes between God's internal eternal speech (kalam nafsi) and the created letters, sounds, and physical objects. The Qur'an as divine speech is eternal; its physical manifestations are created.
This is the key distinction.
4
Note Maturidi's agreement
The passage notes that al-Maturidi and his followers hold the same view, citing al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla. Recognize the consensus among Ash'ari and Maturidi on this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتوسط الأشعري في كلام الله مفرقاً بين كلامه النفسي الذي هو له قديمة قائمة بذاته وبين الحروف المقطعة والأجسام التي يكتب عليها وهي حادثة. يقول ابن عساكر: وكذلك قالت المعتزلة: كلام الله مخلوق مخترع مبتدع. وقالت الحشوية المجسمة: الحروف المقطعة، والأجسام التي يكتب عليها، والألوان التي يكتب بها، وما بين الدفتين كلها قديمة أزلية، فسلك رضي الله عنه طريقة بينهما فقال: القرآن كلام الله قديم، غير مغير ولا يخلوق ولا حادث ولا مبتدع، فأما الحروف المقطعة والأجسام والألوان والأصوات والمحدودات وكل ما في العالم من المكيفات مخلوق مبتدع مخترع. وإلى ذلك أيضاً يذهب الماتريدي والماتريدية، فنقرأ في تبصرة الأدلة للإمام أبي المعين النسفي وهو من أكبر من نصر مذهب الماتريدي: اختلف الناس في كلام الله...
And al-Ash'ari takes a middle position regarding the speech of God, distinguishing between His internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) which is His, eternal, subsisting in His essence, and between the separated letters and the bodies on which they are written, which are originated. Ibn 'Asakir says: Likewise the Mu'tazila said: The speech of God is created, invented, innovated. And the Hashwiyya and Mujassima said: The separated letters, the bodies on which they are written, the colors with which they are written, and what is between the two covers are all pre-existent and eternal. So he, may God be pleased with him, took a way between them and said: The Qur'an is the speech of God, eternal, unchangeable, not created, not originated, not innovated. As for the separated letters, bodies, colors, sounds, limited things, and everything in the world that is qualified, they are created, invented, innovated. And al-Maturidi and the Maturidis also go to that. We read in Tabsirat al-Adilla of Imam Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, who is among the greatest who supported the school of al-Maturidi: People differed concerning the speech of God...
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage presents the middle position of both al-Ash'ari and al-Maturidi on the nature of God's speech (kalam Allah). Ash'ari distinguishes between two aspects: the internal, eternal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) which subsists in God's essence and is uncreated, and the physical expressions such as letters, bodies, colors, and sounds, which are created temporal entities. The Mu'tazila erred by claiming the speech itself is created, while the Hashwiyya and Mujassima erred by claiming even the letters and physical objects of the mushaf are pre-eternal. Ash'ari takes the middle path: the Qur'an as God's speech is eternal and uncreated, but the letters, ink, paper, sounds, etc. are created. The passage then states that al-Maturidi and his followers hold the same view, citing al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla, which reports that people differed on this issue. The quotation is cut off, but the agreement is clear.

Key Terms

الكلام النفسي
كلام أزلي
الحروف المقطعة
مخلوق
تبصرة الأدلة
أبو المعين النسفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the physical letters and paper of the Qur'an are eternal, or that because the letters are created, God's speech itself is created.
Correction: Ash'ari and Maturidi distinguish between the eternal divine attribute of speech (kalam nafsi) and the created physical expressions (letters, sounds, etc.). The Qur'an as God's speech is uncreated, while its material form is created.

Checkpoint

B) The meaning of the Qur'an
C) The letters and sounds of the Qur'an
D) The eternal attribute of speech

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari and Maturidi maintain that God's speech is eternal while the letters and sounds of the Qur'an are created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The Mu'tazila reduced the Qur'an to a mere creation; the Hashwiyya elevated paper and ink to eternity. Which extreme are you more prone to in your heart? Do you treat the mushaf as a sacred object while forgetting the eternal speech it contains, or do you treat it as ordinary because you focus only on its physicality? This week, choose one action to correct that imbalance.
?
This lesson draws a sharp line between the uncreated attribute of speech and the created expressions. Where in your daily life do you blur that line? Do you revere the mushaf's cover more than the meaning in your heart? Today, before opening the mushaf, pause and remind yourself: 'What I hold is created, but what I read is from the Eternal.'

Divine Names Affirmed by Revelation and Reason
Al-Maturidi affirms divine names from revelation and reason and refutes misapplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine names are established by both revelation and reason, and that naming Allah does not imply any resemblance to creation.

Core Idea
Divine names are known from revelation and reason, but never imply likeness to creation.

Al-Maturidi begins by affirming that describing Allah with names like 'Knowing', 'Capable', 'Living' is a right derived from both the Qur'an and rational reflection. He then addresses a misunderstanding: some people mistakenly apply these names to created beings or assume that naming implies similarity. This lesson will clarify al-Maturidi's foundational principle of naming Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once. Notice the key terms like السمع والعقل and التشابه.
The passage has ellipses; focus on the main statements.
2
Identify the two sources
The passage states that describing Allah by these names is a right from السمع (revelation) and العقل (reason). Reflect on why both are needed.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Maturidi criticizes those who direct these names to other than Allah. Why is this a problem? He warns that affirming a name can wrongly imply resemblance.
This leads to his conclusion: Allah is named only by what He named Himself.
4
Grapple with the principle
The passage ends by affirming that Allah is named by His own names and described by His own attributes. This establishes a key theological method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الوصف الله بأنه قادر عالم حي كريم جواد والتسمية بها حق من السمع والعقل جميعاً . قوماً وجهوا تلك الأسماء إلى غيره ظنا منهم ... إلا أن أن في إثبات الاسم تشابهاً بينه وبين كل مسمى... ولكن قد بينا بعد التشابه به لموافقة الاسم ، فهو مسمى بما سمى نفسه ، موصوف بما وصف به نفسه ، ثم يقول الماتريدي في موضع آخر : والله واحد لا شبيه له وإذا ثبت ذا بطل تقدير جميع ما يضاف إليه من الخلق ويوصف به من الصفات ، بما يفهم منه لو أضيف إلى الخلق وفي ذلك ظهور تعنت المشبهة ، وذلك سبب الحاد من ووصف به الحد إنه ظن به ما احتمله الشاهد ... وليس في إثبات الأسماء وتحقيق الصفات تشابه لكنا [أردنا] به ما يُسقط الشبه من قولنا : عالم لا كالعلماء ، وهذا النوع في كل ما نسميه به ونصفه
Then describing Allah as capable, knowing, living, generous, bountiful, and naming Him by these [names] is a right from both revelation and reason together. Some people directed those names to other than Him, supposing from them... except that in affirming the name there is a resemblance between it and every named... But we have already explained after the resemblance to it due to the agreement of the name, so He is named by what He named Himself, described by what He described Himself. Then al-Maturidi says in another place: And Allah is one, no likeness to Him. When this is established, the estimation of all that is attributed to creation and described by attributes is invalidated, by what is understood from it if it were attributed to creation. In that is the manifestation of the obstinacy of the anthropomorphists, and that is the reason for the limit [حد] from and described by the limit, that he supposed about Him what the witnessed [world] allows... And there is no resemblance in affirming the names and verifying the attributes; rather we intend by it what removes the similarity from our saying: Knowing but not like the knowers, and this type in everything we name Him by and describe Him by.
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage is a combined statement from al-Maturidi on naming Allah. It begins by establishing that describing Allah with names like Qadir, 'Alim, Hayy, Karim, Jawwad is obligatory from both sam' (revelation) and 'aql (reason). He then criticizes those who direct these names to other than Allah (likely referring to created beings) out of false assumption. He clarifies that affirming a name does not imply identity or resemblance between the name and the named; rather, the name is merely a sign. Al-Maturidi then emphasizes that Allah is named only by what He named Himself and described by what He described Himself, avoiding any speculation. In the second part, he reinforces divine transcendence: Allah is one with no shabih (likeness). Once this is established, any analogy from creation becomes invalid. The anthropomorphists' obstinacy is exposed, as they attribute to Allah what is only known from the witnessed world. Finally, he reiterates that affirming names and attributes does not entail tashbih; rather, the intention is to negate similarity—for example, 'Alim but not like any other knower. This applies to every name and attribute.

Key Terms
الوصف
التسمية
السمع
العقل
التشابه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that using the same name for Allah and creation implies they share the same attribute in essence.
Correction: Al-Maturidi clarifies that names are merely linguistic signs; they do not imply any likeness. For example, 'Knowing' for Allah is absolutely different from created knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the sources of divine names?
A) Only revelation
B) Only reason
C) Both revelation and reason
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Divine names are established by both revelation and reason, and they do not imply any resemblance to created things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call Allah 'the Generous' (al-Karim), do you ever catch yourself subconsciously comparing His generosity to that of humans? How does Maturidi's warning that the resemblance is only in the name, not in the reality, force you to purge that comparison from your heart?
?
You rely on both revelation and reason to know Allah's names. Is there a name of Allah that your reason struggles to accept or that you feel uncomfortable with? What does that discomfort reveal about your reliance on revelation alone?
?
If a divine name like 'Living' (al-Hayy) is true but does not imply any similarity to our life, how must you change the way you speak about and relate to that name in your daily remembrance and supplication?

Negating Likeness in Divine Attributes
Maturidi negates all likeness and provides the method for affirming attributes without tashbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Maturidi negates any likeness between Allah and creation while still affirming divine attributes.

Core Idea
Allah is one with no likeness; affirming attributes does not imply anthropomorphism.

In this continuation, al-Maturidi reinforces divine transcendence by stating that Allah is one with no shabih (likeness). He then shows how this principle invalidates any analogical estimation from creation. He exposes the anthropomorphists' obstinacy and concludes that affirming names and attributes is done in a way that negates similarity—for example, 'Knowing but not like the knowers.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the phrase 'والله واحد لا شبيه له' and the subsequent deduction.
2
Understand the argument
The initial statement that Allah has no likeness serves as a premise. From this, all attributions from creation become invalid.
Think about why analogical reasoning fails here.
3
Identify the critique
Al-Maturidi mentions 'تعنت المشبهة' – the obstinacy of anthropomorphists. What is their error?
They derive attributes from the witnessed world.
4
Apply the principle
The final sentence gives a key formula for all divine names: affirm the attribute while negating similarity, e.g., 'Alim but not like the knowers.'
This is the method for all names.

Ash'ari's Middle Position on the Speech of Allah
Ibn 'Asakir reports Ash'ari's distinction between eternal attribute and created expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari distinguishes between Allah's eternal attribute of speech and the created physical expressions of the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Allah’s speech is eternal, but its written letters and sounds are created.

This lesson introduces al-Ash'ari's middle position on the nature of the Qur'an and Allah's speech. He avoids the extremes of the Mu'tazila and the Hashwiyya by distinguishing between the eternal internal speech (kalam nafsi) and the created physical letters and bodies. Understanding this distinction clarifies a key Ash'ari doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note that the passage quotes Ibn 'Asakir reporting Ash'ari's view.
The passage is a single paragraph with distinct sections.
2
Identify the two extremes
Look for the positions of the Mu'tazila and the Hashwiyya. The Mu'tazila said all speech is created; the Hashwiyya said even the physical letters are eternal.
3
Understand Ash'ari's distinction
Focus on how Ash'ari splits: internal speech (kalam nafsi) is eternal; the separated letters, bodies, colors, sounds are created.
This is the middle ground between the two extremes.
4
Review the key terms
Note the Arabic terms like الكلام النفسي, الكلام اللفظي, حادثة, قديمة, etc., and their meanings in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتوسط الأشعري في كلام الله مفرقاً بين كلامه النفسي الذي هو له قديمة قائمة بذاته وبين الحروف المقطعة والأجسام التي يكتب عليها وهي حادثة . يقول ابن عساكر : وكذلك قالت المعتزلة : كلام الله مخلوق مخترع مبتدع . وقالت الحشوية المجسمة : الحروف المقطعة ، والأجسام التي يكتب عليها ، والألوان التي يكتب بها ، وما بين الدفتين كلها قديمة أزلية ، فسلك رضي الله عنه طريقة بينهما فقال : القرآن كلام الله قديم، غير مغير ولا يخلوق ولا حادث ولا مبتدع ، فأما الحروف المقطعة والأجسام والألوان والأصوات والمحدودات وكل ما في العالم من المكيفات مخلوق مبتدع مخترع
And al-Ash'ari takes a middle position regarding the speech of Allah, distinguishing between His internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) which is His, eternal, subsisting in His essence, and the separated letters and bodies on which it is written, which are created. Ibn 'Asakir says: Likewise the Mu'tazila said: The speech of Allah is created, invented, originated. And the Hashwiyya mujassima said: The separated letters, the bodies on which it is written, the colors with which it is written, and what is between the two covers are all eternal pre-eternal. So he (Ash'ari) followed a middle way and said: The Qur'an is the speech of Allah, eternal, unchangeable, not created, not temporal, not originated. As for the separated letters, bodies, colors, sounds, limited things, and every qualified thing in the world, they are created, originated, invented.
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage reports al-Ash'ari's position on the nature of Allah's speech as a middle ground between two extremes. Ibn 'Asakir narrates that al-Ash'ari distinguishes between al-kalam al-nafsi (the internal speech), which is an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence, and the physical letters and bodies (e.g., ink on paper) which are created. The Mu'tazila had claimed that all of Allah's speech is created, while the Hashwiyya (anthropomorphists) claimed that even the physical letters and materials are eternal. Al-Ash'ari splits: the Qur'an as Allah's speech is eternal, uncreated, and not temporal, but its physical manifestations—letters, bodies, colors, sounds, and all limited things—are created. This avoids both the Mu'tazili denial of an eternal attribute and the Hashwi attribution of eternity to created objects.

الكلام اللفظي

ابتدا

Correction: Ash'ari distinguishes: the eternal attribute of speech is uncreated, but the physical letters and objects are created.

?

Nasafi on the Eternal Speech of Allah
Nasafi defines kalam as eternal attribute and explains created expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's distinction between the eternal attribute of speech and the created expressions of the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is one eternal attribute, while the recited words are created indications.

In this passage, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi tackles a central theological debate: is the Qur'an eternal or created? He affirms the Ahl al-Sunna position that divine speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence, while the Arabic, Hebrew, or Syriac expressions we read are created and point to that eternal speech. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Let the author's flow come through.
Notice the two-part structure: first the disagreement and basic stance, then the detailed depiction.
2
Identify the key distinction
Identify what Nasafi says about the nature of divine speech (eternal attribute) and what he says about the expressions (created letters and sounds).
The expressions are 'cibarat' that indicate the eternal attribute.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters: it preserves the eternity of Allah's attribute while affirming the createdness of the recited Qur'an as a temporal event.
This answers the opponents' confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلف الناس في كلام الله أقديم هو أم محدث . قال أهل الحق : إن كلام الله تعالى صفة له أزلية ليست من جنس الحروف والأصرات ، وهي صفة قائمة بذاته منافية للسكون الطفولية والخرس وغير ذلك ، والله متكلم بها آمر ناه مخبر ، وهذه العبارات وفي موضع آخر يقول النسفي : ثم نشتغل بتصوير المسألة بيننا وبين الخصوم في القرآن ليتبين بذلك اندفاع أكثر أسئلتهم التي يموهون بها على الضعفة فنقول : إن كلام الله تعالى صفة قائمة بذات الله تعالى ليست من جنس الحروف والأصوات ، وهو صفة واحدة ، وهو أمر به ، نهى عما نهى عنه ، خبر عما أخبر عنه ، وهو صفة أزلية . ثم هذه العبارات العربية أو العبرية أو السورية عبارات عنه ، وهو يتأدى بها وهذه العبارات حروف وأصوات ، وهي محدثة مخلوقة في محلها دلالات على الكلام الذي هو الصفة الأزلية لله تعالى، لها أبعاض وأجزاء وأنصاف وأعشار وغير ذلك ، وهي أنفسها مختلفة ، أنفسها مختلفة ، وكلام الله تعالى واحد ليس بمجتزى ولا بمختلف
People differed concerning the speech of Allah: is it eternal or temporal? The people of truth said: The speech of Allah is an eternal attribute of His, not of the genus of letters and sounds, and it is an attribute subsisting in His essence, negating [?] silence, childhood, and muteness, and others. Allah is speaking with it, commanding, forbidding, informing. And these expressions... And in another place al-Nasafi says: Then we begin by depicting the issue between us and the opponents concerning the Qur'an so that thereby the repulsion of most of their questions which they confuse the weak with becomes clear. So we say: The speech of Allah is an attribute subsisting in the essence of Allah, not of the genus of letters and sounds, and it is one attribute, and it is a command, a prohibition of what He forbade, news of what He informed, and it is an eternal attribute. Then these Arabic, Hebrew, or Syriac expressions are expressions of it, and it is conveyed through them. And these expressions are letters and sounds, and they are created, created in their places, indications of the speech which is the eternal attribute of Allah. They have parts, sections, halves, tenths, and others, and they themselves are different, different. And the speech of Allah is one, not divisible and not different.
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage presents Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi's exposition on the nature of divine speech. It begins by stating the disagreement: is Allah's speech eternal or temporal? 'Ahl al-Haqq' (the people of truth, i.e., Ahl al-Sunna) affirm that it is an eternal attribute, not composed of letters or sounds, subsisting in Allah's essence. It negates silence, childhood (i.e., inability to speak), and muteness. Allah is speaking with it as commander, prohibitor, informer. Nasafi then says that to counter the opponents' misleading questions, we must depict the issue clearly: Allah's speech is one eternal attribute, which is the command, prohibition, and information. The Arabic, Hebrew, or Syriac expressions are expressions of that attribute, conveyed through them. These expressions are letters and sounds that are created (muhdatha makhluqa) in their places, serving as indications (dilalat) of the eternal attribute. They have parts and divisions, while the divine attribute is one, indivisible, and unchanging. This upholds the distinction between the eternal attribute and the created recitation.

Key Terms
كلام الله
صفة أزلية
حروف
أصوات
عبارات
دلالات
محدثة
مخلوقة
متكلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The recited Qur'an (Arabic text) is eternal and uncreated.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that the Arabic expressions are created letters and sounds that indicate the eternal attribute; the eternal attribute itself is not composed of letters or sounds.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the nature of the Arabic, Hebrew, or Syriac expressions of the Qur'an?
A) They are eternal
B) They are created and indicate the eternal speech
C) They are part of Allah's essence
D) They are uncreated but divisible

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is one indivisible eternal attribute, while the recited Qur'an consists of created letters and sounds that indicate it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you feel that you are directly hearing Allah's eternal speech, or do you recognize that the words you utter are created indications of something infinitely greater? How would your heart change if you truly internalized this distinction?
?
The passage teaches that Allah's speech is one attribute that commands, forbids, and informs. Does your obedience to the commands and avoidance of prohibitions flow from a deep connection to this single attribute, or do you treat them as separate obligations? Will you strive to see the unity behind the diverse injunctions?
?
If Allah's speech is eternal and not composed of letters, then your recitation of the Qur'an is a finite act pointing to the infinite. Does that make you humble before the words? Are you willing to increase your reverence for every letter you recite, knowing it is a signpost to the eternal? What concrete change will you make in your recitation practice starting today?

The Two Contradictory Syllogisms on the Quran
Maturidi presents two paradoxical syllogisms on divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two syllogisms that form the core of the debate on whether the Quran is eternal or created.

Core Idea
Two syllogisms, two opposite conclusions—only one can be valid.

This lesson presents two contradictory syllogisms that capture the central theological debate over the nature of Allah's speech. One syllogism concludes that the Quran is eternal; the other concludes it is originated. The tension lies in the premises, which different schools resolve in distinct ways.

Guided Path
1
Read the two syllogisms carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Let the structure of each syllogism become clear.
Notice that both syllogisms start with a premise about speech.
2
Identify the premises of the first syllogism
List the minor and major premises. The conclusion follows necessarily if both premises are true.
Minor: Speech is an attribute. Major: All attributes are eternal.
3
Identify the premises of the second syllogism
Do the same for the second syllogism. Notice how the premises lead to the opposite conclusion.
Minor: Speech is composed of letters. Major: Composed things are originated.
4
Reflect on the contradiction
Ask yourself: both conclusions cannot be true simultaneously. Which premise might be false?
The remainder of the original text will answer this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الماتريدي والأشعري: كلام الله صفة له. كل ما هو صفة له فهو قديم. فكلام الله قديم. كلام الله مؤلف من حروف مترتبة متعاقبة في الوجود. وكل ما هو كذلك فهو حادث. فكلام الله حادث. فأهل السنة من الماتريدية والأشعرية منعوا صغرى القياس الثاني وهي: 'كلام الله مؤلف من حروف مترتبة متعاقبة في الوجود'. والحنابلة منعوا كبرى القياس الثاني وهي: 'أن كل ما هو مؤلف من حروف وأصوات مترتبة فهو حادث'، وذهبوا إلى أن كلام الله تعالى مؤلف من أصوات وحروف مترتبة، وأنها قديمة قائمة بذاته تعالى. والمعتزلة منعوا صغرى القياس الأول وهي: 'أن كلام الله صفة له'، وذهبوا إلى أن كلام الله تعالى مؤلف من أصوات وحروف مترتبة قائم بغيره تعالى، وأن معنى كونه متكلماً كونه موجداً لتلك الحروف والأصوات في جسم كاللوح المحفوظ أو جبريل أو النبي أو غيرها كشجرة موسى، وأن الكلام النفسي غير ثابت لأنه غير معقول. والكرامية منعوا كبرى القياس الأول وهي: 'كل صفة له فهو قديم'، وذهبوا إلى أن كلامه تعالى صفة له مؤلف من الحروف والأصوات الحادثة وقائمة بذاته.
Al-Maturidi and al-Ash'ari: The speech of God is an attribute for Him. Everything that is an attribute for Him is eternal. So the speech of God is eternal. The speech of God is composed of arranged letters successive in existence. And everything that is such is originated. So the speech of God is originated. So the Ahl al-Sunnah from the Maturidis and Ash'aris denied the minor premise of the second syllogism, which is: 'The speech of God is composed of arranged letters successive in existence.' And the Hanbalis denied the major premise of the second syllogism, which is: 'Everything that is composed of arranged letters and sounds is originated,' and they held that the speech of God Most High is composed of arranged sounds and letters, and that they are eternal subsisting in His essence. And the Mu'tazila denied the minor premise of the first syllogism, which is: 'The speech of God is an attribute for Him,' and they held that the speech of God Most High is composed of arranged sounds and letters subsisting in other than Him, and that the meaning of His being speaking is His bringing into existence those letters and sounds in a body like the Preserved Tablet or Gabriel or the Prophet or other things like the tree of Moses, and that the inner speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) is not established because it is not intelligible. And the Karramiyya denied the major premise of the first syllogism, which is: 'Every attribute for Him is eternal,' and they held that His speech is an attribute for Him composed of originated letters and sounds subsisting in His essence.
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage presents two contradictory syllogisms that encapsulate the debate over whether Allah's speech (the Quran) is eternal or created. The first syllogism argues that since Allah's speech is an attribute of His, and every attribute of His is eternal, then His speech is eternal. The second syllogism argues that since Allah's speech is composed of arranged letters succeeding each other in existence, and everything composed like that is originated (hadith), then His speech is originated. The passage then explains which premise each school denies to resolve the contradiction. The Ash'aris and Maturidis deny the minor premise of the second syllogism—they do not accept that Allah's speech is literally composed of letters and sounds. The Hanbalis deny the major premise of the second syllogism, holding that composition with letters and sounds can be eternal. The Mu'tazila deny the minor premise of the first syllogism, rejecting that Allah's speech is an attribute subsisting in His essence; instead, they say it is created speech existing in some other locus. The Karramiyya deny the major premise of the first syllogism, asserting that some attributes (like speech) can be originated while still subsisting in Allah's essence. Thus, each school's position is defined by which logical premise they reject.

Key Terms
قياس
صغرى
كبرى
كلام الله
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the first syllogism?
A) Speech is composed of letters
B) Speech is eternal
C) Speech is originated
D) Speech is a divine attribute

Key Takeaway
Two syllogisms with opposite conclusions force theologians to reject at least one premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you feel its eternal nature or its temporal descent? Which feeling transforms your heart more deeply?
?
If one of these syllogisms must be false, what does that say about your own attempts to fully capture Allah's speech with human logic? Are you willing to accept that some truths are beyond your grasp?
?
You have just seen two logically constructed arguments lead to opposite conclusions. How often do you rely on your own reasoning in matters of faith, and when was the last time you surrendered a logical question to simply trusting Allah's word?

Sunni Responses: Maturidis, Ash'aris, and Hanbalis
Response of Ahl al-Sunnah (Maturidis, Ash'aris, Hanbalis) to the syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sunni schools (Maturidis, Ash'aris, and Hanbalis) deny different premises to maintain the eternity of the Quran.

Core Idea
Maturidis/Ash'aris deny composition; Hanbalis deny that composition implies temporality.

The two syllogisms are contradictory, so each school must reject at least one premise. This lesson examines how the three main Sunni groups—Maturidis, Ash'aris, and Hanbalis—each resolve the contradiction while affirming the Quran's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two syllogisms from the previous lesson
Remember the first syllogism (eternal) and second (originated). We now see which premise each school rejects.
2
Learn the Maturidi/Ash'ari position
They deny the minor premise of the second syllogism—that speech is composed of letters.
For them, the Quran is an eternal attribute, not a physical text.
3
Learn the Hanbali position
They deny the major premise of the second syllogism—that composition implies temporality.
They affirm that the letters/sounds are eternal, subsisting in Allah's essence.
4
Reflect on the unity within diversity
Both groups affirm the Quran's eternity, but via different logical moves. Notice how each preserves orthodoxy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the Karramiyya denied the major premise of the first syllogism, which is: 'Every attribute for Him is eternal,' and they held that His speech is an attribute for Him composed of originated letters and sounds subsisting in His essence.

Mu'tazila and Karramiyya Responses
Response of Mu'tazila and Karramiyya to the two syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya reject different premises to conclude that the Quran is created and eternal respectively.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila deny speech as attribute; Karramiyya deny that all attributes are eternal.

The contradictory syllogisms force each school to reject at least one premise. This lesson covers the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya. The Mu'tazila deny that speech is an attribute of Allah, thus accepting the second syllogism's conclusion that the Quran is created. The Karramiyya deny that all divine attributes are eternal, claiming that speech is an attribute but originated.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two syllogisms from the first lesson
Focus on the first syllogism (speech is attribute → eternal). Both Mu'tazila and Karramiyya reject it.
2
Learn the Mu'tazila view
They deny the minor premise: that speech is an attribute of Allah. They believe the Quran is created speech in a created locus.
They reject inner speech (kalam nafsi) as unintelligible.
3
Learn the Karramiyya view
They deny the major premise: that all attributes are eternal. They accept speech as an attribute but say it is originated.
For them, speech consists of created letters/sounds that subsist in Allah's essence.
4
Compare with Sunni positions
Note that both Mu'tazila and Karramiyya deny premises of the first syllogism, while Sunnis deny premises of the second.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

Can you identify an attribute of Allah that you have difficulty accepting, and what step will you take to affirm it fully?
?
The Karramiyya affirmed that speech is an attribute of Allah but insisted it is created. Are you ever guilty of holding a middle position that seems logical but contradicts the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna? How can you ensure your beliefs are not merely the product of your own reasoning but are firmly rooted in the Qur'an and Sunnah?

4 min
The Agreement of Ahl al-Sunna on the Seven Essential Attributes
States the agreement of Ash'aris and Maturidis on the seven essential attributes and their definition as eternal meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools agree on the seven essential attributes as eternal meanings subsisting in God's essence, distinct yet not separate from His essence.

Core Idea
The seven attributes are eternal meanings, neither identical to God nor other than Him.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi clarifies the common ground between the two main Sunni theological schools regarding Allah's attributes. While Ash'aris and Maturidis may differ in secondary issues, they are united in affirming the seven essential attributes as real and eternal meanings that subsist in God's essence. This lesson explores that core agreement and its implications for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the author's style and flow.
The passage uses technical terms like 'معاني قديمة'.
2
Identify the seven attributes
List the seven attributes mentioned: knowledge, power, will, life, hearing, sight, speech. Notice they are called 'essential attributes' (الصفات الذاتية).
3
Understand the ontological status
Focus on the key phrase: 'ليست هي ذاته ولا غير ذاته' (neither identical to His essence nor other than it). This is the precise formulation of the Sunni position.
This avoids both anthropomorphism and pure negation.
4
Recognize the agreement
Note the statement that all Ahl al-Sunna (both Ash'aris and Maturidis) agree on these seven attributes. This shows the unity of Sunni theology on this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأشعرية والماتريدية : إنهم المخالفون في الأصول لسائر الفرق مخالفة كثيرة كمسألة الكسب والرؤية بلا كيفية ، وجواز رؤية أعمى الصين [؟] وجواز رؤية كل موجود ، وإسناد جميع الموجودات إلى الله تعالى ، وكونه موصوفاً بصفات ليست عين الذات ولا غيرها ... وما وقع بين أهل السنة من مخالفات فتلك في التفاريع نعم ، يتفق أهل السنة في الأصول ، ويختلفون بعد ذلك في الفروع . يتفقون على اثبات الصفات الذاتية على أنها معاني قديمة قائمة بذات الله، ليست هي ذاته ولا غير ذاته .. فالله عندهم عالم يعلم أو عالم وله علم وعلمه معنى قديم قائم بذاته زائد على ذاته، ليس هو ذاته ولا غيرها . وكذلك الحال في قدرته وإرادته وحياته وسمعه وبصره وكلامه . وجميع أهل السنة من ماتريدية وأشعرية متفقون على هذه الصفات السبعة
The Ash'aris and the Maturidis: they are the ones who oppose all other sects in fundamentals with much opposition, such as the issue of acquisition (kasb) and [God's] vision without modality, and the permissibility of a blind person seeing [God] [؟], and the permissibility of seeing every existent, and attributing all existents to God Almighty, and that He is described by attributes that are not identical to the essence nor other than it... And whatever disagreements occurred among Ahl al-Sunna, those are in the branches. Yes, Ahl al-Sunna agree in fundamentals, and then they differ in the branches. They agree on affirming the essential attributes (al-sifat al-dhatiyya) as eternal meanings subsisting in God's essence, neither identical to His essence nor other than His essence. So God according to them is knowing with knowledge or knowing and He has knowledge, and His knowledge is an eternal meaning subsisting in His essence, additional to His essence, not identical to it nor other than it. And likewise in the case of His power, will, life, hearing, sight, and speech. And all Ahl al-Sunna, whether Maturidis or Ash'aris, agree on these seven attributes.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage defines the common ground between the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools. It first identifies them as those who oppose other sects on many fundamental issues, listing examples such as human acquisition (kasb), the vision of God without modality, and the affirmation of attributes that are neither identical to God's essence nor distinct from it in a way that implies multiplicity. The passage then clarifies that internal disagreements among Ahl al-Sunna are only in branches (furu‘), not in fundamentals (usul). The core agreement is on the seven essential attributes (life, knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, speech) which are described as eternal meanings subsisting in God's essence. These attributes are additional to the essence but not separate or identical. The text gives the example of knowledge: God is knowing 'with knowledge' or 'has knowledge' — the knowledge is an eternal meaning, not the essence itself nor something other than it. The same applies to all seven attributes.

Key Terms
أهل السنة
الصفات الذاتية
معاني قديمة
قائمة بذات الله
زائد على الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ash'aris and Maturidis disagree on the reality of God's attributes.
Correction: This passage shows that they agree on the seven essential attributes as eternal meanings subsisting in God's essence, neither identical nor other.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the seven attributes in relation to God's essence according to Ahl al-Sunna?
A) They are identical to the essence
B) They are completely separate from the essence
C) They are eternal meanings subsisting in the essence, additional to it, neither identical nor other
D) They are created entities

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna (Ash'aris and Maturidis) agree that Allah's seven essential attributes are eternal meanings subsisting in His essence, additional to it, yet neither identical nor other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah's knowledge is an eternal meaning subsisting in His essence, not identical nor other, how does that affect your awareness that He knows your innermost thoughts? Will you live today with the conviction that nothing is hidden from Him?
?
This lesson affirms that Allah has seven essential attributes: knowledge, power, will, life, hearing, sight, and speech. Which of these attributes have you neglected in your worship? What specific action will you take today to deepen your connection with Allah through that attribute?
?
The precise wording 'neither identical nor other' guards against exaggerating or diminishing Allah's attributes. Are there areas in your life where you tend to reduce Allah's attributes to mere words without reality, or where you attribute creation-like qualities to Him? How will you correct that?

Disagreement on the Attribute of Baqa' (Survival)
Explains differing views on whether survival is an additional attribute or identical to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the intra-Sunni disagreement over whether God's everlastingness is by an additional attribute of survival (baqa') or by His essence itself, and the unique position of Imam Nur al-Din al-Sabuni.

Core Idea
Even within agreed schools, divine attributes can spark internal debates that cross school lines.

After settling on the essential attributes, theologians turned to others like survival (baqa'). Both Ash'aris and Maturidis affirm God's everlastingness, but they disagree on its nature: is it an added attribute or identical to the essence? This passage highlights that disagreement and reveals a surprising cross-school alignment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the agreed premise
Both Ash'aris and Maturidis agree that God is everlasting (baqi). This is the starting point of the discussion.
2
Grasp the core question
The disagreement is whether God is everlasting by an additional attribute (baqa') or by His essence itself (bi-dhatihi).
The passage rephrases this as: is survival a meaning additional to existence, or identical to existence in the second moment?
3
Identify the Ash'ari position
Al-Ash'ari and most of his followers hold that survival is an attribute additional to the essence, like knowledge or life.
4
Notice the Maturidi majority view and al-Sabuni's exception
Most Maturidis hold that God is everlasting by His essence, not by an additional attribute. However, Imam Nur al-Din al-Sabuni supported the Ash'ari view, opposing his fellow Maturidis.
This shows that individual scholars may diverge from their school's majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكنهم مختلفون بعد ذلك حول اثبات عدد آخر من كصفة البقاء وصفة التكوين . فالأشعرية والماتريدية متفقون على أن الله تعالى باق ، ولكنهم مختلفون في معنى بقائه : هل هو باق ببقاء ، وبقاؤه صفة له زائدة على ذاته قائمة بذاته، أم أنه باق بذاته لا ببقاء ؟ بعبارة أخرى : هل البقاء أو استمرار الذات في الوجود معنى زائد على وجود الذات أم أنه هو عين وجود الذات في الزمان الثاني ؟ ذهب الأشعري وأكثر أصحابه إلى أن البقاء صفة زائدة على الذات مثلها في ذلك مثل العلم والحياة وسائر صفات المعاني . ونصر قول الأشعري نفر الإمام نور الدين الصابوني مخالفاً بذلك جمهور أصحابه من الماتريدية
But they differ after that concerning the affirmation of a number of other [attributes], such as the attribute of survival (baqa') and the attribute of bringing into existence (takwin). For the Ash'aris and the Maturidis agree that God Almighty is everlasting (baqi), but they differ regarding the meaning of His everlastingness: is He everlasting by a survival (bi-baqa'), and His survival is an attribute for Him additional to His essence subsisting in His essence, or is He everlasting by His essence (bi-dhatihi), not by a survival? In other words: is survival, i.e., the continuation of the essence in existence, a meaning additional to the existence of the essence, or is it identical to the existence of the essence in the second moment? Al-Ash'ari and most of his followers held that survival is an attribute additional to the essence, like knowledge, life, and the other attributes of meanings. And [someone] supported the statement of al-Ash'ari, namely Imam Nur al-Din al-Sabuni, thereby opposing the majority of his fellows among the Maturidis.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage presents a specific intra-Sunni disagreement regarding the attribute of survival (baqa'), which is not among the seven essential attributes agreed upon. After noting that both schools agree on God's everlastingness, the passage frames the disagreement as a question: Is God everlasting by an additional attribute of survival (baqa'), or is He everlasting by His essence itself? The text clarifies this by asking whether survival as continuation of existence in time is an extra meaning beyond the essence's existence or simply the same existence in a second moment. The Ash'ari position, held by al-Ash'ari and most of his followers, is that survival is indeed an additional attribute, akin to knowledge and life. Interestingly, the passage notes that Imam Nur al-Din al-Sabuni, a prominent Maturidi scholar, sided with al-Ash'ari on this point, opposing the majority of his Maturidi colleagues. This shows that not all disagreements neatly follow school boundaries.

Key Terms
البقاء
باق ببقاء
باق بذاته
التكوين
نور الدين الصابوني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Maturidis hold the same view that God is everlasting by His essence, not by an additional attribute.
Correction: This passage shows that Imam Nur al-Din al-Sabuni, a Maturidi, sided with the Ash'ari view that survival is an additional attribute, demonstrating that individual scholars may differ from their school's majority.

Checkpoint
On the issue of survival (baqa'), which position did Imam Nur al-Din al-Sabuni take?
A) He agreed with the majority of Maturidis that God is everlasting by His essence
B) He supported al-Ash'ari's view that survival is an additional attribute
C) He denied that God is everlasting
D) He remained silent on the issue

Key Takeaway
The disagreement over survival shows that even within Sunni schools, there are nuanced debates, and individual scholars may differ from their school's majority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about scholars like al-Sabuni who held a view opposite to his school's majority, do you feel challenged to think independently about your own beliefs? How do you balance respect for tradition with personal conviction in matters of faith?
?
If God's survival is by His essence and not by an external attribute, what does that imply about your dependence on Him for every moment of your existence? Do you live with that constant awareness?
?
The debate over baqa' shows that even the greatest minds disagree on details of God's nature. Does this unsettle you, or does it humble you? How might this humility change the way you approach those who differ with you in theology?

Agreement on the Seven Essential Attributes in Ahl al-Sunna
Describes the agreement of Ahl al-Sunna on the seven essential attributes and their nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ahl al-Sunna (Ash'aris and Maturidis) agree on the seven essential attributes and their nature as eternal meanings subsisting in God's essence, neither identical to nor other than it.

Core Idea
The seven essential attributes are eternal meanings in God's essence, distinct but not separate.

This passage clarifies the fundamental agreement among Ahl al-Sunna on the divine attributes. While differences exist in secondary matters, the core doctrine of the seven essential attributes is unanimously affirmed. Here, the text defines these attributes as eternal meanings that subsist in God's essence, establishing a key theological principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the main claim about agreement on fundamentals rather than branches.
Notice the emphasis on unity among Ash'aris and Maturidis.
2
Identify the seven attributes
List the seven essential attributes mentioned: knowledge, power, will, life, hearing, sight, and speech.
These are known as the 'sifat dhatiyya'.
3
Understand the definition
Focus on the phrase 'معاني قديمة قائمة بذات الله، ليست هي ذاته ولا غير ذاته' – this is the precise definition of the attributes' relationship to the essence.
The attributes are superadded (za'id) but not separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأشعرية والماتريدية : إنهم المخالفون في الأصول لسائر الفرق مخالفة كثيرة كمسألة الكسب والرؤية بلا كيفية ، وجواز رؤية أعمى الصين بقة أندلس ، وجواز رؤية كل موجود ، وإسناد جميع الموجودات إلى الله تعالى ، وكونه موصوفاً بصفات ليست عين الذات ولا غيرها ... وما وقع بين أهل السنة وما وقع بين أهل السنة من مخالفات فتلك في التفاريع نعم ، يتفق أهل السنة في الأصول ، ويختلفون بعد ذلك في الفروع . يتفقون على اثبات الصفات الذاتية على أنها معاني قديمة قائمة بذات الله ، ليست هي ذاته ولا غير ذاته .. فالله عندهم عالم يعلم أو عالم وله علم" وعلمه معنى قديم قائم بذاته زائد على ذاته ، ليس هو ذاته ولا غيرها . وكذلك الحال في قدرته وإرادته وحياته وسمعه وبصره وكلامه . وجميع أهل السنة من ماتريدية وأشعرية متفقون الصفات. على هذه الصفات السبعة
The Ash'aris and the Maturidis: they are the ones who oppose other sects in fundamental principles with much opposition, like the issue of acquisition (al-kasb) and vision (al-ru'ya) without modality, and the permissibility of seeing a blind man of China [in] a spot of Andalusia, and the permissibility of seeing every existent, and attributing all existents to God Almighty, and His being described with attributes that are neither identical to the essence nor other than it... And what occurs between Ahl al-Sunna and what occurs between Ahl al-Sunna of differences, those are in the branches. Yes, Ahl al-Sunna agree on the fundamentals, and differ after that in the branches. They agree on affirming the essential attributes as being eternal meanings subsisting in God's essence, not identical to His essence nor other than His essence... Thus God according to them is knowing with knowledge or knowing and He has knowledge, and His knowledge is an eternal meaning subsisting in His essence, superadded to His essence, not identical to His essence nor other than it. And similarly regarding His power, will, life, hearing, sight, and speech. And all Ahl al-Sunna from Maturidis and Ash'aris agree on these seven attributes.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of the common ground between Maturidis and Ash'aris. It first states that these two groups are distinct from other sects in many doctrinal issues (like kasb, vision, etc.) but then emphasizes that differences among Ahl al-Sunna are only in secondary matters (furu'), while they agree on the fundamentals (usul). The core agreement is on the seven essential attributes: knowledge, power, will, life, hearing, sight, and speech. They define these attributes as eternal meanings (ma'ani qadima) that subsist in God's essence, superadded to the essence, but neither identical to nor other than the essence. The passage gives the example of knowledge: God is 'alim (knowing) with knowledge, or 'alim and He has knowledge, and that knowledge is an eternal meaning. The same applies to the other attributes. The conclusion is that all Ahl al-Sunna, whether Maturidi or Ash'ari, agree on these seven attributes.

Key Terms
الصفات الذاتية
المعاني القديمة
السبعة صفات
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'aris and Maturidis disagree on the seven attributes themselves.
Correction: The passage states they all agree on these seven essential attributes as defined.

Checkpoint

A) They are identical to God's essence.
B) They are other than God's essence.
C) They are eternal meanings subsisting in God's essence, neither identical to nor other than it.
D) They are created and temporal.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna unanimously affirm the seven essential attributes as eternal meanings subsisting in God's essence, neither identical nor other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the teaching that God's attributes are neither identical to His essence nor separate from it challenge the way you normally think about God? Do you tend to reduce His attributes to mere concepts, or do you inadvertently imagine them as separate entities? How can you internalize this balanced understanding in your daily worship?
?
The Ahl al-Sunna unanimously affirm that God's knowledge, power, and life are eternal meanings subsisting in His essence. If you truly believed this, how would it transform your trust in His knowledge of your struggles, His power to change your situation, and His life that never ends? Are there areas where you act as if God is unaware or powerless?
?
When you speak of God being 'knowing' or 'powerful,' do you really mean that He has a real attribute of knowledge and power, or are you just saying words? This lesson insists that the attributes are superadded realities. Could your prayer life change if you meditated on the reality of each attribute before you call upon Him?

The Disagreement on the Attribute of Baqa'
Presents the disagreement on baqa' and the two views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the Ash'ari and Maturidi positions on whether God's eternity (baqa') is an additional attribute or identical with His essence.

Core Idea
God's eternity: a superadded attribute or identical with His essence?

Both Ash'aris and Maturidis affirm that God is eternal (baqi), but they diverge on the nature of that eternity. This passage presents the key question: Is His eternity due to an attribute of baqa' superadded to His essence, or is He eternal by His essence alone? Understanding this disagreement is essential for grasping the nuanced approaches to divine attributes within Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get a sense of the question being debated. Note that both groups agree that God is eternal.
Pay attention to the phrasing 'باق ببقاء' vs 'باق بذاته'.
2
Identify the two opposing views
The passage clearly states al-Ash'ari's view (baqa' is a superadded attribute) and refers to the Maturidi majority who reject this. Locate where each view is expressed.
Also notice that Imam al-Sabuni supported the Ash'ari view despite being Maturidi.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this distinction matters: if baqa' is superadded, it raises questions about divine simplicity and the relationship between God's essence and attributes. The Maturidi position (eternal by essence) avoids multiplying attributes.
This connects to broader debates about God's attributes being identical with or distinct from His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالأشعرية والماتريدية متفقون على أن الله تعالى باق ، ولكنهم مختلفون في معنى بقائه : هل هو باق ببقاء ، وبقاؤه صفة له زائدة على ذاته قائمه بذاته، أم أنه باق بذاته لا ببقاء ؟ بعبارة أخرى : هل البقاء أو استمرار الذات في الوجود معنى زائد على وجود الذات أم أنه هو عين وجود عين وجود الذات في الزمان الثاني ؟ ذهب الأشعري وأكثر أصحابه إلى أن البقاء صفة زائدة على الذات مثلها في ذلك مثل العلم والحياة وسائر صفات المعاني . ونصر قول الأشعري نفر الإمام نور الدين الصابوني مخالفاً بذلك جمهور أصحابه من الماتريدية
For the Ash'aris and the Maturidis agree that God Almighty is eternal (baqi), but they differ regarding the meaning of His eternity: Is He eternal by an eternity, His eternity being an attribute superadded to His essence, subsisting in His essence, or is He eternal by His essence without an eternity? In other words, is eternity or the continuation of the essence in existence a meaning superadded to the existence of the essence, or is it identical with the existence of the essence in the second time? Al-Ash'ari and most of his followers held that eternity is an attribute superadded to the essence, like knowledge, life, and all the attributes of meanings. And a group supported the saying of al-Ash'ari, among them Imam Nur al-Din al-Sabuni, opposing thereby the majority of his fellows from the Maturidis.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal dispute between Ash'aris and Maturidis over the attribute of baqa' (eternity). Both groups agree that God is eternal (baqi), but they disagree on the meaning of that eternity. The passage frames the disagreement as a question: Is God eternal by an attribute of baqa' that is superadded to His essence, or is He eternal by His essence without any additional attribute? It clarifies by asking whether the continuation of the essence in existence is an extra meaning or identical with the existence itself in a second moment of time. Al-Ash'ari and most of his followers (including the later support from Imam Nur al-Din al-Sabuni, though he was Maturidi) hold that baqa' is a superadded attribute like knowledge and life. The majority of Maturidis, however, reject that baqa' is an additional attribute, holding that God is eternal by His essence.

Key Terms
البقاء
صفة زائدة
باق بذاته
صفات المعاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That both groups agree on all attributes; they differ specifically on whether baqa' is an additional attribute.
Correction: While both affirm God's eternity, they differ on the nature of that eternity – whether it is a superadded attribute or identical with the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the position of Al-Ash'ari and most of his followers regarding the attribute of baqa'?
A) Baqa' is identical with the essence.
B) Baqa' is a superadded attribute.
C) Baqa' does not exist.
D) Baqa' is a temporal attribute.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school holds that baqa' is a superadded attribute, while the Maturidi school (majority) holds that God is eternal by His essence without an additional attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson shows al-Sabuni standing against his school's majority for a truth he held about God's eternity. Are you willing to hold fast to a truth about God even when it means standing alone against those you respect? Ask yourself: what truth about God's attributes might you be compromising for the sake of conformity?
?
Both schools affirm God's eternal existence. In the face of your own mortality, how does the reality of God's eternity—whether by essence or attribute—challenge you to live with greater urgency and hope? What is one concrete change you will make today because God is eternal?
?
The disagreement over baqa' may seem abstract, but it concerns the very nature of God's permanence. Does your heart truly rest in the certainty that God is eternal, or do you secretly rely on temporary things? Commit to a moment today where you consciously affirm to yourself: 'God is eternal, and my life depends on that alone.'

Maturidi and Ash'ari on the Attribute of Takwin
Maturidi affirmation of takwin as real attribute with proof and contrast to Ash'ari view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that takwin is a real eternal attribute distinct from qudra, and the logical proof for this distinction from al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
Takwin, not qudra, brings possible things from non-existence to existence.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid presents a foundational doctrine of Maturidi theology: the distinction between the attribute of power (qudra) and the attribute of causation (takwin). While the Ash'aris see takwin as a contingent attribute identical with the act, the Maturidis affirm it as an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence. The text then provides a logical proof from al-Nasafi that power alone cannot account for bringing things into existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analysing. Notice the initial contrast between the Maturidi and Ash'ari views on the nature of takwin.
The Arabic text contains some OCR uncertainties; focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify the two positions
The Maturidis consider takwin a 'haqiqiyya qadima' (real eternal) attribute, while the Ash'aris consider it 'idafiyya haditha' (contingent originated). Find these descriptions in the text.
3
Analyse the proof from al-Nasafi
Al-Nasafi argues that power (qudra) only entails possibility (maqdur), not actual existence. If power caused existence, then God would only be 'powerful' over the world, not its 'creator'. This necessitates a separate attribute for bringing into existence.
This proof is the core of the Maturidi argument for takwin.
